<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097025294">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:#f9f9f9}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1092331828">@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .contains-special-characters{width:22em}}</style><div class="side-box metadata side-box-right contains-special-characters noprint selfref">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><img alt="" data-file-height="384" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="30" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Farsi.svg/40px-Farsi.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Farsi.svg/60px-Farsi.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Farsi.svg/80px-Farsi.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/Persian_alphabet" title="Persian alphabet">Persian text</a>.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:Arabic" title="Help:Arabic">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a>.</div></div>
</div>
<p><b>JalÄl al-DÄ«n Muá¸¥ammad RÅ«mÄ«</b> (<a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="fa">Ø¬ÙØ§ÙâØ§ÙØ¯ÛÙ ÙØ­ÙØ¯ Ø±ÙÙÛ</span>), also known as <b>JalÄl al-DÄ«n Muá¸¥ammad BalkhÄ«</b> (<span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">Ø¬ÙØ§ÙâØ§ÙØ¯ÛÙ ÙØ­ÙØ¯ Ø¨ÙØ®Ù</span></span>), <b>MevlÃ¢nÃ¢</b>/<b>MawlÄnÄ</b> (<a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="fa">ÙÙÙØ§ÙØ§</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a>â</small>'our master') and <b>MevlevÃ®</b>/<b>MawlawÄ«</b> (<a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="fa">ÙÙÙÙÛ</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a>â</small>'my master'), but more popularly known simply as <b>Rumi</b> (30 September 1207Â â 17 December 1273), was a 13th-century <a href="/wiki/Persians" title="Persians">Persian</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EI_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-EI-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-lewis-12">[12]</a></sup> poet, <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faqih" title="Faqih">faqih</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ulama" title="Ulama">Islamic scholar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maturidi" title="Maturidi">Maturidi</a> theologian and <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi</a> <a href="/wiki/Mysticism" title="Mysticism">mystic</a> originally from <a href="/wiki/Greater_Khorasan" title="Greater Khorasan">Greater Khorasan</a> in <a href="/wiki/Greater_Iran" title="Greater Iran">Greater Iran</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-lewis-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Rumi's influence transcends national borders and ethnic divisions: <a href="/wiki/Persians" title="Persians">Iranians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tajiks" title="Tajiks">Tajiks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">Turks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cappadocian_Greeks" title="Cappadocian Greeks">Greeks</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pashtun_people" title="Pashtun people">Pashtuns</a>, other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_Asian_peoples" title="Central Asian peoples">Central Asian Muslims</a>, as well as Muslims of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> have greatly appreciated his spiritual legacy for the past seven centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nasr1_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nasr1-14">[14]</a></sup> His poems have been widely translated into many of the world's languages and transposed into various formats. Rumi has been described as the "most popular poet"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-Haviland_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-Haviland-15">[15]</a></sup> and the "best selling poet" in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rumi's works are written mostly in <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>, but occasionally he also used <a href="/wiki/Old_Anatolian_Turkish" title="Old Anatolian Turkish">Turkish</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Annemarie_Schimmel_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Annemarie_Schimmel-18">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Franklin_Lewis_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Franklin_Lewis-19">[19]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Cappadocian_Greek" title="Cappadocian Greek">Greek</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dedes1993_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dedes1993-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> in his verse. His <i><a href="/wiki/Masnavi" title="Masnavi">Masnavi</a></i> (<i>Mathnawi</i>), composed in <a href="/wiki/Konya" title="Konya">Konya</a>, is considered one of the greatest poems of the Persian language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C.E._Bosworth_p._391_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-C.E._Bosworth_p._391-24">[24]</a></sup> His works are widely read today in their original language across <a href="/wiki/Greater_Iran" title="Greater Iran">Greater Iran</a> and the Persian-speaking world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Translations of his works are very popular, most notably in <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>, the United States and South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> His poetry has influenced not only <a href="/wiki/Persian_literature" title="Persian literature">Persian literature</a>, but also the literary traditions of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turkish_language" title="Ottoman Turkish language">Ottoman Turkish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chagatai_language" title="Chagatai language">Chagatai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pashto_language" title="Pashto language">Pashto</a> languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span></h2>
<p>He is most commonly called <i>Rumi</i> in English. His full name is given by his contemporary Sipahsalar as <i>Muhammad bin Muhammad bin al-Husayn al-Khatibi al-Balkhi al-Bakri</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">ÙØ­ÙØ¯ Ø¨Ù ÙØ­ÙØ¯ Ø¨Ù Ø§ÙØ­Ø³ÙÙ Ø§ÙØ®Ø·ÙØ¨Ù Ø§ÙØ¨ÙØ®Ù Ø§ÙØ¨ÙØ±Ù</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> He is more commonly known as <i>JalÄl ad-DÄ«n Muá¸¥ammad RÅ«mÄ«</i> (<span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">Ø¬ÙØ§ÙâØ§ÙØ¯ÛÙ ÙØ­ÙØ¯ Ø±ÙÙÛ</span></span>). <i>Jalal ad-Din</i> is an <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> name meaning "Glory of the Faith". <i>BalkhÄ«</i> and <i>RÅ«mÄ«</i> are his <i><a href="/wiki/Nisba_(onomastics)" title="Nisba (onomastics)">nisbas</a></i>, meaning, respectively, "from <a href="/wiki/Balkh" title="Balkh">Balkh</a>" and "from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/R%C3%BBm" title="RÃ»m">RÃ»m</a>" ('Roman,' what European history now calls <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Anatolia" title="Byzantine Anatolia">Byzantine Anatolia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>). According to the authoritative Rumi biographer <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Lewis" title="Franklin Lewis">Franklin Lewis</a> of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">University of Chicago</a>, "[t]he Anatolian peninsula which had belonged to the Byzantine, or eastern Roman empire, had only relatively recently been conquered by Muslims and even when it came to be controlled by Turkish Muslim rulers, it was still known to Arabs, Persians and Turks as the geographical area of Rum. As such, there are a number of historical personages born in or associated with Anatolia known as Rumi, a word borrowed from Arabic literally meaning 'Roman,' in which context Roman refers to subjects of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> or simply to people living in or things associated with <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> He was also known as "Mullah of Rum" (<span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">ÙÙØ§Û Ø±ÙÙ</span></span> <i>mullÄ-yi RÅ«m</i> or <span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">ÙÙØ§Û Ø±ÙÙÛ</span></span> <i>mullÄ-yi RÅ«mÄ«</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>He is widely known by the <a href="/wiki/Sobriquet" title="Sobriquet">sobriquet</a> <i>MawlÄnÄ</i>/<i>MolÄnÄ</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EI_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-EI-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyclopaedia1991_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-encyclopaedia1991-5">[5]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="fa">ÙÙÙØ§ÙØ§</span> <small>Persian pronunciation:Â </small><span class="IPA" lang="fa-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Persian" title="Help:IPA/Persian">[moulÉËnÉ]</a></span>) in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and popularly known as <span title="Turkish-language text"><i lang="tr">MevlÃ¢nÃ¢</i></span> in Turkey. <i>MawlÄnÄ</i> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">ÙÙÙØ§ÙØ§</span></span>) is a term of <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> origin, meaning "our master".
</p><p>The term <span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">ÙÙÙÙÛ</span></span> <i>MawlawÄ«</i>/<i>Mowlavi</i> (Persian) and <span title="Turkish-language text"><i lang="tr">Mevlevi</i></span> (Turkish), also of Arabic origin, meaning "my master", is also frequently used for him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jalal_al-Din_Rumi,_Showing_His_Love_for_His_Young_Disciple_Hussam_al-Din_Chelebi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="555" decoding="async" height="396" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Jalal_al-Din_Rumi%2C_Showing_His_Love_for_His_Young_Disciple_Hussam_al-Din_Chelebi.jpg/220px-Jalal_al-Din_Rumi%2C_Showing_His_Love_for_His_Young_Disciple_Hussam_al-Din_Chelebi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Jalal_al-Din_Rumi%2C_Showing_His_Love_for_His_Young_Disciple_Hussam_al-Din_Chelebi.jpg/330px-Jalal_al-Din_Rumi%2C_Showing_His_Love_for_His_Young_Disciple_Hussam_al-Din_Chelebi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Jalal_al-Din_Rumi%2C_Showing_His_Love_for_His_Young_Disciple_Hussam_al-Din_Chelebi.jpg/440px-Jalal_al-Din_Rumi%2C_Showing_His_Love_for_His_Young_Disciple_Hussam_al-Din_Chelebi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jalal_al-Din_Rumi,_Showing_His_Love_for_His_Young_Disciple_Hussam_al-Din_Chelebi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jalal ad-Din Rumi gathers <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufi" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> mystics.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ahmad_ibn_Hajji_Abi_Bakr_al-Katib_-_Double-page_Illuminated_Frontispiece_-_Walters_W6252B_-_Full_Page.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="1181" decoding="async" height="335" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ahmad_ibn_Hajji_Abi_Bakr_al-Katib_-_Double-page_Illuminated_Frontispiece_-_Walters_W6252B_-_Full_Page.jpg/220px-Ahmad_ibn_Hajji_Abi_Bakr_al-Katib_-_Double-page_Illuminated_Frontispiece_-_Walters_W6252B_-_Full_Page.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ahmad_ibn_Hajji_Abi_Bakr_al-Katib_-_Double-page_Illuminated_Frontispiece_-_Walters_W6252B_-_Full_Page.jpg/330px-Ahmad_ibn_Hajji_Abi_Bakr_al-Katib_-_Double-page_Illuminated_Frontispiece_-_Walters_W6252B_-_Full_Page.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ahmad_ibn_Hajji_Abi_Bakr_al-Katib_-_Double-page_Illuminated_Frontispiece_-_Walters_W6252B_-_Full_Page.jpg/440px-Ahmad_ibn_Hajji_Abi_Bakr_al-Katib_-_Double-page_Illuminated_Frontispiece_-_Walters_W6252B_-_Full_Page.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ahmad_ibn_Hajji_Abi_Bakr_al-Katib_-_Double-page_Illuminated_Frontispiece_-_Walters_W6252B_-_Full_Page.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Double-page illuminated frontispiece, 1st book (daftar) of the Collection of poems (<a href="/wiki/Masnavi" title="Masnavi"><i>Masnavi-i ma'navi</i></a>), 1461 manuscript</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bowl_of_Reflections,_early_13th_century.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1161" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Bowl_of_Reflections%2C_early_13th_century.jpg/220px-Bowl_of_Reflections%2C_early_13th_century.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Bowl_of_Reflections%2C_early_13th_century.jpg/330px-Bowl_of_Reflections%2C_early_13th_century.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Bowl_of_Reflections%2C_early_13th_century.jpg/440px-Bowl_of_Reflections%2C_early_13th_century.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bowl_of_Reflections,_early_13th_century.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Bowl of Reflections</i> with Rumi's poetry, early 13th century. <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Museum" title="Brooklyn Museum">Brooklyn Museum</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span></h3>
<p>Rumi was born to native Persian-speaking parents,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Annemarie_Schimmel_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Annemarie_Schimmel-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Franklin_Lewis_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Franklin_Lewis-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nasr2_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nasr2-35">[35]</a></sup> originally from the <a href="/wiki/Balkh" title="Balkh">Balkh</a>, which at the time was part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khwarezmian_Empire" title="Khwarezmian Empire">Khwarezmian Empire</a>, but is now in present-day <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>. He was born either in <a href="/wiki/Vakhsh,_Tajikistan" title="Vakhsh, Tajikistan">Wakhsh</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balkh_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Balkh-4">[4]</a></sup> a village on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vakhsh_River" title="Vakhsh River">Vakhsh River</a> in present-day <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balkh_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Balkh-4">[4]</a></sup> or in the city of Balkh, in present-day Afghanistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNESCO_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNESCO-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNESDOC_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNESDOC-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Greater Balkh was at that time a major centre of Persian culture<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C.E._Bosworth_p._391_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-C.E._Bosworth_p._391-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nasr2_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nasr2-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a> had developed there for several centuries. The most important influences upon Rumi, besides his father, were the Persian poets <a href="/wiki/Attar_of_Nishapur" title="Attar of Nishapur">Attar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sanai" title="Sanai">Sanai</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Rumi expresses his appreciation: "Attar was the spirit, Sanai his eyes twain, And in time thereafter, Came we in their train"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> and mentions in another poem: "Attar has traversed the seven cities of Love, We are still at the turn of one street".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> His father was also connected to the spiritual lineage of <a href="/wiki/Najm_al-Din_Kubra" title="Najm al-Din Kubra">Najm al-Din Kubra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nasr1_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nasr1-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rumi lived most of his life under the <a href="/wiki/Persianate_society" title="Persianate society">Persianate</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Seljuk_dynasty" title="Seljuk dynasty">Seljuk</a> <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Rum" title="Sultanate of Rum">Sultanate of Rum</a>, where he produced his works<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> and died in 1273<span class="nowrap">Â </span>AD. He was buried in <a href="/wiki/Konya" title="Konya">Konya</a>, and his shrine became a place of pilgrimage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Upon his death, his followers and his son <a href="/wiki/Sultan_Walad" title="Sultan Walad">Sultan Walad</a> founded the <a href="/wiki/Mevlevi_Order" title="Mevlevi Order">Mevlevi Order</a>, also known as the Order of the Whirling Dervishes, famous for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufi_dance" title="Sufi dance">Sufi dance</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/Sama_(Sufism)" title="Sama (Sufism)">Sama</a> ceremony. He was laid to rest beside his father, and over his remains a shrine was erected. A hagiographical account of him is described in Shams ud-Din Ahmad AflÄki's <i>ManÄqib ul-ÄrifÄ«n</i> (written between 1318 and 1353). This biography needs to be treated with care as it contains both legends and facts about Rumi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howisit_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-howisit-46">[46]</a></sup> For example, Professor <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Lewis" title="Franklin Lewis">Franklin Lewis</a> of the University of Chicago, author of the most complete biography on Rumi, has separate sections for the <a href="/wiki/Hagiography" title="Hagiography">hagiographical</a> biography of Rumi and the actual biography about him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hagiographer1_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-hagiographer1-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood_and_emigration">Childhood and emigration</span></h3>
<p>Rumi's father was BahÄ ud-DÄ«n Walad, a theologian, jurist and a <a href="/wiki/Mysticism" title="Mysticism">mystic</a> from Balkh, who was also known by the followers of Rumi as Sultan al-Ulama or "Sultan of the Scholars". According to Sultan Walad's <i>Ibadetname</i> and Shamsuddin Aflaki (c.1286 to 1291), Rumi was a descendant of <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Some modern scholars, however, reject this claim and state it does not hold on closer examination. The claim of maternal descent from the <a href="/wiki/Khwarazmshah" title="Khwarazmshah">Khwarazmshah</a> for Rumi or his father is also seen as a non-historical hagiographical tradition designed to connect the family with royalty, but this claim is rejected for chronological and historical reasons. The most complete genealogy offered for the family stretches back to six or seven generations to famous Hanafi jurists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hagiographer1_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-hagiographer1-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hagiographer2_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-hagiographer2-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hagiographer3_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-hagiographer3-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>We do not learn the name of Baha al-Din's mother in the sources, only that he referred to her as "MÄmi" (colloquial Persian for MÄma),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hagiographer4_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-hagiographer4-51">[51]</a></sup> and that she was a simple woman who lived to the 1200s. The mother of Rumi was Mu'mina KhÄtÅ«n. The profession of the family for several generations was that of Islamic preachers of the relatively liberal <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a> <a href="/wiki/Maturidi" title="Maturidi">Maturidi</a> school, and this family tradition was continued by Rumi (see his Fihi Ma Fih and Seven Sermons) and Sultan Walad (see Ma'rif Waladi for examples of his everyday sermons and lectures).
</p><p>When the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol" title="Mongol">Mongols</a> invaded Central Asia sometime between 1215 and 1220, Baha ud-Din Walad, with his whole family and a group of disciples, set out westwards. According to hagiographical account which is not agreed upon by all Rumi scholars, Rumi encountered one of the most famous mystic Persian poets, <a href="/wiki/Attar_of_Nishapur" title="Attar of Nishapur">Attar</a>, in the Iranian city of <a href="/wiki/Nishapur" title="Nishapur">Nishapur</a>, located in the province of KhorÄsÄn. Attar immediately recognized Rumi's spiritual eminence. He saw the father walking ahead of the son and said, "Here comes a sea followed by an ocean."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Attar gave the boy his <i>AsrÄrnÄma</i>, a book about the entanglement of the soul in the material world. This meeting had a deep impact on the eighteen-year-old Rumi and later on became the inspiration for his works.
</p><p>From Nishapur, Walad and his entourage set out for <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>, meeting many of the scholars and Sufis of the city.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> From Baghdad they went to <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hejaz</a> and performed the <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">pilgrimage</a> at <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>. The migrating caravan then passed through <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malatya" title="Malatya">Malatya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erzincan" title="Erzincan">Erzincan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sivas" title="Sivas">Sivas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kayseri" title="Kayseri">Kayseri</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nigde" title="Nigde">Nigde</a>. They finally settled in <a href="/wiki/Karaman" title="Karaman">Karaman</a> for seven years; Rumi's mother and brother both died there. In 1225, Rumi married Gowhar Khatun in Karaman. They had two sons: Sultan Walad and Ala-eddin Chalabi. When his wife died, Rumi married again and had a son, Amir Alim Chalabi, and a daughter, Malakeh Khatun.
</p><p>On 1 May 1228, most likely as a result of the insistent invitation of <a href="/wiki/Kayqubad_I" title="Kayqubad I">'AlÄ' ud-DÄ«n Key-QobÄd</a>, ruler of Anatolia, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baha-ud-Din_Naqshband_Bukhari" title="Baha-ud-Din Naqshband Bukhari">Baha' ud-Din</a> came and finally settled in Konya in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> within the westernmost territories of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seljuk_Sultanate_of_R%C3%BBm" title="Seljuk Sultanate of RÃ»m">Seljuk Sultanate of RÃ»m</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education_and_encounters_with_Shams-e_Tabrizi">Education and encounters with Shams-e Tabrizi</span></h3>
<p>Baha' ud-Din became the head of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madrassa" title="Madrassa">madrassa</a> (religious school) and when he died, Rumi, aged twenty-five, inherited his position as the Islamic molvi. One of Baha' ud-Din's students, Sayyed Burhan ud-Din Muhaqqiq Termazi, continued to train Rumi in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shariah" title="Shariah">Shariah</a> as well as the <a href="/wiki/Tariqa" title="Tariqa">Tariqa</a>, especially that of Rumi's father. For nine years, Rumi practised Sufism as a disciple of Burhan ud-Din until the latter died in 1240 or 1241. Rumi's public life then began: he became an Islamic Jurist, issuing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatwas" title="Fatwas">fatwas</a> and giving sermons in the mosques of Konya. He also served as a Molvi (Islamic teacher) and taught his adherents in the madrassa.
</p><p>During this period, Rumi also travelled to <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a> and is said to have spent four years there.
</p><p>It was his meeting with the dervish <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shams-e_Tabrizi" title="Shams-e Tabrizi">Shams-e Tabrizi</a> on 15 November 1244 that completely changed his life. From an accomplished teacher and jurist, Rumi was transformed into an ascetic.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tomb_of_Shams_Tabrizi_9.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="864" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Tomb_of_Shams_Tabrizi_9.JPG/220px-Tomb_of_Shams_Tabrizi_9.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Tomb_of_Shams_Tabrizi_9.JPG/330px-Tomb_of_Shams_Tabrizi_9.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Tomb_of_Shams_Tabrizi_9.JPG/440px-Tomb_of_Shams_Tabrizi_9.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tomb_of_Shams_Tabrizi_9.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tomb shrine of Shams Tabrizi, <a href="/wiki/Khoy" title="Khoy">Khoy</a></div></div></div>
<p>Shams had travelled throughout the Middle East searching and praying for someone who could "endure my company". A voice said to him, "What will you give in return?" Shams replied, "My head!" The voice then said, "The one you seek is Jalal ud-Din of Konya." On the night of 5 December 1248, as Rumi and Shams were talking, Shams was called to the back door. He went out, never to be seen again. It is rumoured that Shams was murdered with the connivance of Rumi's son, 'Ala' ud-Din; if so, Shams indeed gave his head for the privilege of mystical friendship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rumi's love for, and his bereavement at the death of, Shams found their expression in an outpouring of lyric poems, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diwan-e_Shams-e_Tabrizi" title="Diwan-e Shams-e Tabrizi"><i>Divan-e Shams-e Tabrizi</i></a>. He himself went out searching for Shams and journeyed again to Damascus. There, he realised:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Why should I seek? I am the same as<br/>
He. His essence speaks through me.<br/>
I have been looking for myself!<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life_and_death">Later life and death</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maulana_Jelaledin_Muhammad_Rumi_in_konya.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3116" data-file-width="4680" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Maulana_Jelaledin_Muhammad_Rumi_in_konya.jpg/220px-Maulana_Jelaledin_Muhammad_Rumi_in_konya.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Maulana_Jelaledin_Muhammad_Rumi_in_konya.jpg/330px-Maulana_Jelaledin_Muhammad_Rumi_in_konya.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Maulana_Jelaledin_Muhammad_Rumi_in_konya.jpg/440px-Maulana_Jelaledin_Muhammad_Rumi_in_konya.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maulana_Jelaledin_Muhammad_Rumi_in_konya.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tomb shrine of Rumi, <a href="/wiki/Konya" title="Konya">Konya</a></div></div></div>
<p>Mewlana had been spontaneously composing <i><a href="/wiki/Ghazal" title="Ghazal">ghazals</a></i> (Persian poems), and these had been collected in the <i>Divan-i Kabir</i> or Diwan Shams Tabrizi. Rumi found another companion in Salaá¸¥ ud-Din-e Zarkub, a goldsmith. After Salah ud-Din's death, Rumi's scribe and favourite student, <a href="/wiki/Husam_al-Din_Chalabi" title="Husam al-Din Chalabi">Hussam-e Chalabi</a>, assumed the role of Rumi's companion. One day, the two of them were wandering through the Meram vineyards outside Konya when Hussam described to Rumi an idea he had had: "If you were to write a book like the <i>IlÄhÄ«nÄma</i> of Sanai or the <i>Mantiq ut-Tayr</i> of 'Attar, it would become the companion of many troubadours. They would fill their hearts from your work and compose music to accompany it." Rumi smiled and took out a piece of paper on which were written the opening eighteen lines of his <i>Masnavi</i>, beginning with:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Listen to the reed and the tale it tells,<br/>
How it sings of separation...<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Hussam implored Rumi to write more. Rumi spent the next twelve years of his life in Anatolia dictating the six volumes of this masterwork, the <i>Masnavi</i>, to Hussam.
</p><p>In December 1273, Rumi fell ill; he predicted his own death and composed the well-known <i>ghazal</i>, which begins with the verse:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>How doest thou know what sort of king I have within me as companion?<br/>
Do not cast thy glance upon my golden face, for I have iron legs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Rumi died on 17 December 1273 in <a href="/wiki/Konya" title="Konya">Konya</a>. His death was mourned by the diverse community of Konya, with local Christians and Jews joining the crowd that converged to bid farewell as his body was carried through the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mojaddedi-19_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mojaddedi-19-58">[58]</a></sup> Rumi's body was interred beside that of his father, and a splendid shrine, the <i>YeÅil TÃ¼rbe</i> (Green Tomb, ÙØ¨Ù Ø§ÙØ®Ø¶Ø±Ø§Ø¡; today the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mevlana_Museum" title="Mevlana Museum">MevlÃ¢na Museum</a>), was erected over his place of burial. His epitaph reads:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>When we are dead, seek not our tomb in the earth,
but find it in the hearts of men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Georgian princess and Seljuq queen <a href="/wiki/Gurju_Khatun" title="Gurju Khatun">Gurju Khatun</a> was a close friend of Rumi. She was the one who sponsored the construction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mevlana_Museum" title="Mevlana Museum">his tomb</a> in <a href="/wiki/Konya" title="Konya">Konya</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The 13th century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mevlana_Museum" title="Mevlana Museum">MevlÃ¢na Mausoleum</a>, with its mosque, dance hall, schools and living quarters for dervishes, remains a destination of pilgrimage to this day, and is probably the most popular pilgrimage site to be regularly visited by adherents of every major religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mojaddedi-19_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mojaddedi-19-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Teachings">Teachings</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shams_ud-Din_Tabriz_1502-1504_BNF_Paris.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="418" decoding="async" height="316" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Shams_ud-Din_Tabriz_1502-1504_BNF_Paris.jpg/220px-Shams_ud-Din_Tabriz_1502-1504_BNF_Paris.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Shams_ud-Din_Tabriz_1502-1504_BNF_Paris.jpg/330px-Shams_ud-Din_Tabriz_1502-1504_BNF_Paris.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e4/Shams_ud-Din_Tabriz_1502-1504_BNF_Paris.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shams_ud-Din_Tabriz_1502-1504_BNF_Paris.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page of a copy c. 1503 of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diwan-e_Shams-e_Tabriz-i" title="Diwan-e Shams-e Tabriz-i">Diwan-e Shams-e Tabriz-i</a></i>. See <a href="/wiki/Rumi_ghazal_163" title="Rumi ghazal 163">Rumi ghazal 163</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Like other mystic and Sufi poets of Persian literature, Rumi's poetry speaks of love which infuses the world. Rumi's teachings also express the tenets summarized in the Quranic verse which Shams-e Tabrizi cited as the essence of prophetic guidance: "Know that âThere is no god but He,â and ask forgiveness for your sin" (Q. 47:19). In the interpretation attributed to Shams, the first part of the verse commands the humanity to seek knowledge of <i><a href="/wiki/Tawhid" title="Tawhid">tawhid</a></i> (oneness of God), while the second instructs them to negate their own existence. In Rumi's terms, <i>tawhid</i> is lived most fully through love, with the connection being made explicit in his verse that describes love as "that flame which, when it blazes up, burns away everything except the Everlasting Beloved."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Rumi's longing and desire to attain this ideal is evident in the following poem from his book the <a href="/wiki/Masnavi" title="Masnavi">Masnavi</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1109925960">.mw-parser-output .verse_translation .translated{padding-left:2em}@media only screen and (max-width:43.75em){.mw-parser-output .verse_translation.wrap_when_small td{display:block;padding-left:0}.mw-parser-output .verse_translation.wrap_when_small .translated{padding-left:0}}</style>
</p>
<table class="verse_translation wrap_when_small" role="presentation" style="margin-left:1em;">
<tbody><tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td><div class="poem" lang="" style="font-style:roman;text-align:right">
<p><span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">Ø§Ø² Ø¬ÙØ§Ø¯Û ÙÙØ±Ø¯Ù Ù ÙØ§ÙÛ Ø´Ø¯Ù<br/>
ÙØ² ÙÙØ§ ÙÙØ±Ø¯Ù Ø¨Ù Ø­ÛÙØ§Ù Ø¨Ø±Ø²Ø¯Ù<br/>
ÙÙØ±Ø¯Ù Ø§Ø² Ø­ÛÙØ§ÙÛ Ù Ø¢Ø¯Ù Ø´Ø¯Ù<br/>
Ù¾Ø³ ÚÙ ØªØ±Ø³Ù Ú©Û Ø² ÙØ±Ø¯Ù Ú©Ù Ø´Ø¯ÙØ<br/>
Ø­ÙÙÙÙ Ø¯ÛÚ¯Ø± Ø¨ÙÛØ±Ù Ø§Ø² Ø¨Ø´Ø±<br/>
ØªØ§ Ø¨Ø±Ø¢Ø±Ù Ø§Ø² ÙÙØ§Ø¦Ú© Ø¨Ø§Ù Ù Ù¾Ø±<br/>
ÙØ² ÙÙÚ© ÙÙ Ø¨Ø§ÛØ¯Ù Ø¬Ø³ØªÙ Ø² Ø¬Ù<br/>
Ú©Ù Ø´ÛØ¡ ÙØ§ÙÚ© Ø§ÙØ§ ÙØ¬ÙÙ<br/>
Ø¨Ø§Ø± Ø¯ÛÚ¯Ø± Ø§Ø² ÙÙÚ© Ù¾Ø±Ø§Ù Ø´ÙÙ<br/>
Ø¢ÙÚ Ø§ÙØ¯Ø± ÙÙÙ ÙØ§ÛØ¯ Ø¢Ù Ø´ÙÙ<br/>
Ù¾Ø³ Ø¹Ø¯Ù Ú¯Ø±Ø¯Ù Ø¹Ø¯Ù ÚÙÙ Ø§Ø±ØºÙÙÙ<br/>
Ú¯ÙÛØ¯Ù Ú©Ù Ø§ÙØ§ Ø§ÙÛÙ Ø±Ø§Ø¬Ø¹ÙÙ</span></span>
</p>
</div>
</td>
<td class="translated"><div class="poem">
<p>I died to the mineral state and became a plant,<br/>
I died to the vegetal state and reached animality,<br/>
I died to the animal state and became a man,<br/>
Then what should I fear? I have never become less from dying.<br/>
At the next charge (forward) I will die to human nature,<br/>
So that I may lift up (my) head and wings (and soar) among the angels,<br/>
And I must (also) jump from the river of (the state of) the angel,<br/>
Everything perishes except His Face,<br/>
Once again I will become sacrificed from (the state of) the angel,<br/>
I will become that which cannot come into the imagination,<br/>
Then I will become non-existent; non-existence says to me (in tones) like an organ,<br/>
<a href="/wiki/Inna_Lillahi_wa_inna_ilayhi_raji%27un" title="Inna Lillahi wa inna ilayhi raji'un">Truly, to Him is our return</a>.
</p>
</div>
</td></tr>
<tr style="display: none;">
<td>
</td>
<td style="padding-left: 2em;">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <i>Masnavi</i> weaves fables, scenes from everyday life, Qur'anic revelations and exegesis, and metaphysics into a vast and intricate tapestry.
</p><p>Rumi believed passionately in the use of music, poetry and dance as a path for reaching God. For Rumi, music helped devotees to focus their whole being on the divine and to do this so intensely that the soul was both destroyed and resurrected. It was from these ideas that the practice of <a href="/wiki/Sufi_whirling" title="Sufi whirling">whirling Dervishes</a> developed into a ritual form. His teachings became the base for the order of the Mevlevi, which his son Sultan Walad organised. Rumi encouraged <a href="/wiki/Sama_(Sufism)" title="Sama (Sufism)">Sama</a>, listening to music and turning or doing the sacred dance. In the Mevlevi tradition, <i>samÄÊ¿</i> represents a mystical journey of spiritual ascent through mind and love to the Perfect One. In this journey, the seeker symbolically turns towards the truth, grows through love, abandons the ego, finds the truth and arrives at the Perfect. The seeker then returns from this spiritual journey, with greater maturity, to love and to be of service to the whole of creation without discrimination with regard to beliefs, races, classes and nations.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In other verses in the <i>Masnavi</i>, Rumi describes in detail the universal message of love:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The lover's cause is separate from all other causes<br/>
Love is the astrolabe of God's mysteries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Rumi's favourite musical instrument was the <a href="/wiki/Ney" title="Ney">ney</a> (reed flute).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-Haviland_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-Haviland-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_works">Major works</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meeting_of_Jalal_al-Din_Rumi_and_Molla_Shams_al-Din.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1776" data-file-width="1332" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Meeting_of_Jalal_al-Din_Rumi_and_Molla_Shams_al-Din.jpg/220px-Meeting_of_Jalal_al-Din_Rumi_and_Molla_Shams_al-Din.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Meeting_of_Jalal_al-Din_Rumi_and_Molla_Shams_al-Din.jpg/330px-Meeting_of_Jalal_al-Din_Rumi_and_Molla_Shams_al-Din.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Meeting_of_Jalal_al-Din_Rumi_and_Molla_Shams_al-Din.jpg/440px-Meeting_of_Jalal_al-Din_Rumi_and_Molla_Shams_al-Din.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meeting_of_Jalal_al-Din_Rumi_and_Molla_Shams_al-Din.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> era manuscript depicting Rumi and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shams-e_Tabrizi" title="Shams-e Tabrizi">Shams-e Tabrizi</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Rumi's poetry is often divided into various categories: the quatrains (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruba%27i" title="Ruba'i">rubayÄt</a></i>) and odes (<i><a href="/wiki/Ghazal" title="Ghazal">ghazal</a></i>) of the <i>Divan</i>, the six books of the <i>Masnavi</i>. The prose works are divided into The Discourses, The Letters, and the <i>Seven Sermons</i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poetic_works">Poetic works</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Turkey.Konya021.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Turkey.Konya021.jpg/220px-Turkey.Konya021.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Turkey.Konya021.jpg/330px-Turkey.Konya021.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Turkey.Konya021.jpg/440px-Turkey.Konya021.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Turkey.Konya021.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center>Maá¹­nawÄ«ye Ma'nawÄ«<br/> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mevlana_Museum" title="Mevlana Museum">MevlÃ¢na Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Konya" title="Konya">Konya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> </center></div></div></div>
<ul><li>Rumi's best-known work is the <i><a href="/wiki/Masnavi" title="Masnavi">Maá¹­nawÄ«ye Ma'nawÄ«</a></i> (<i>Spiritual Couplets</i>; <span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">ÙØ«ÙÙÛ ÙØ¹ÙÙÛ</span></span>). The six-volume poem holds a distinguished place within the rich tradition of Persian Sufi literature, and has been commonly called "the Quran in Persian".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Many commentators have regarded it as the greatest mystical poem in world literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> It contains approximately 27,000 lines,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> each consisting of a couplet with an internal rhyme.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mojaddedi-19_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mojaddedi-19-58">[58]</a></sup> While the mathnawi genre of poetry may use a variety of different metres, after Rumi composed his poem, the metre he used became the mathnawi metre <i>par excellence</i>. The first recorded use of this metre for a mathnawi poem took place at the Nizari Ismaili fortress of Girdkuh between 1131â1139. It likely set the stage for later poetry in this style by mystics such as Attar and Rumi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></li></ul>
<ul><li>Rumi's other major work is the <i>DÄ«wÄn-e KabÄ«r</i> (<i>Great Work</i>) or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diwan-e_Shams-e_Tabrizi" title="Diwan-e Shams-e Tabrizi">DÄ«wÄn-e Shams-e TabrÄ«zÄ«</a></i> (<i>The Works of Shams of <a href="/wiki/Tabriz" title="Tabriz">Tabriz</a></i>; <span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">Ø¯ÛÙØ§Ù Ø´ÙØ³ ØªØ¨Ø±ÛØ²Û</span></span>), named in honour of Rumi's master <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shams_Tabraiz" title="Shams Tabraiz">Shams</a>. Besides approximately 35000 Persian couplets and 2000 Persian quatrains,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> the Divan contains 90 Ghazals and 19 quatrains in Arabic,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> a couple of dozen or so couplets in Turkish (mainly <a href="/wiki/Macaronic_language" title="Macaronic language">macaronic</a> poems of mixed Persian and Turkish)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> and 14 couplets in Greek (all of them in three macaronic poems of Greek-Persian).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dedes1993_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dedes1993-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prose_works">Prose works</span></h3>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fihi_ma_fihi" title="Fihi ma fihi"><i>Fihi Ma Fihi</i></a> (<i>In It What's in It</i>, Persian: ÙÛÙ ÙØ§ ÙÛÙ) provides a record of seventy-one talks and lectures given by Rumi on various occasions to his disciples. It was compiled from the notes of his various disciples, so Rumi did not author the work directly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> An English translation from the Persian was first published by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.J._Arberry" title="A.J. Arberry">A.J. Arberry</a> as <i>Discourses of Rumi</i> (New York: Samuel Weiser, 1972), and a translation of the second book by Wheeler Thackston, <i>Sign of the Unseen</i> (Putney, VT: Threshold Books, 1994). The style of the <i>Fihi ma fihi</i> is colloquial and meant for middle-class men and women, and lack the sophisticated wordplay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>MajÄles-e Sab'a</i> (<i>Seven Sessions</i>, Persian: <span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">ÙØ¬Ø§ÙØ³ Ø³Ø¨Ø¹Ù</span></span>) contains seven Persian sermons (as the name implies) or lectures given in seven different assemblies. The sermons themselves give a commentary on the deeper meaning of Qur'an and <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">Hadith</a>. The sermons also include quotations from poems of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sana%27i" title="Sana'i">Sana'i</a>, <a href="/wiki/Attar_of_Nishapur" title="Attar of Nishapur">'Attar</a>, and other poets, including Rumi himself. As AflakÄ« relates, after Shams-e TabrÄ«zÄ«, Rumi gave sermons at the request of notables, especially SalÄh al-DÄ«n ZarkÅ«b. The style of Persian is rather simple, but quotation of Arabic and knowledge of history and the Hadith show Rumi's knowledge in the Islamic sciences. His style is typical of the genre of lectures given by Sufis and spiritual teachers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Makatib</i> (<i>The Letters</i>, Persian: ÙÚ©Ø§ØªÛØ¨) or <i>Maktubat</i> (<span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">ÙÚ©ØªÙØ¨Ø§Øª</span></span>) is the <a href="/wiki/Letter_collection" title="Letter collection">collection of letters</a> written in Persian by Rumi to his disciples, family members, and men of state and of influence. The letters testify that Rumi kept very busy helping family members and administering a community of disciples that had grown up around them. Unlike the Persian style of the previous two mentioned works (which are lectures and sermons), the letters are consciously sophisticated and epistolary in style, which is in conformity with the expectations of correspondence directed to nobles, statesmen and kings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_outlook">Religious outlook</span></h2>
<p>Rumi belongs to the class of Islamic philosophers which include <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Arabi" title="Ibn Arabi">Ibn Arabi</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> These transcendental philosophers are often studied together in traditional schools of <a href="/wiki/Irfan" title="Irfan">irfan</a>, philosophy and theosophy throughout the Muslim world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rumi embeds his theosophy (transcendental philosophy) like a string through the beads of his poems and stories. His main point and emphasis is the unity of being.
</p><p>It is undeniable that Rumi was a Muslim scholar and took Islam seriously. Nonetheless, the depth of his spiritual vision extended beyond narrow understanding sectarian concerns. One quatrain reads:
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1109925960" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>
</p>
<table class="verse_translation" role="presentation" style="margin-left:1em;">
<tbody><tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td><div class="poem" lang="" style="font-style:roman;text-align:right">
<p><span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">Ø¯Ø± Ø±Ø§Ù Ø·ÙØ¨ Ø¹Ø§ÙÙ Ù Ø¯ÛÙØ§ÙÙ ÛÚ©Û Ø§Ø³Øª<br/>
Ø¯Ø± Ø´ÛÙÙâÛ Ø¹Ø´Ù Ø®ÙÛØ´ Ù Ø¨ÛÚ¯Ø§ÙÙ ÛÚ©Û Ø§Ø³Øª<br/>
Ø¢Ù Ø±Ø§ Ú©Ù Ø´Ø±Ø§Ø¨ ÙØµÙ Ø¬Ø§ÙØ§Ù Ø¯Ø§Ø¯ÙØ¯<br/>
Ø¯Ø± ÙØ°ÙØ¨ Ø§Ù Ú©Ø¹Ø¨Ù Ù Ø¨ØªØ®Ø§ÙÙ ÛÚ©Û Ø§Ø³Øª</span></span>
</p>
</div>
</td>
<td class="translated"><div class="poem">
<p>On the seeker's path, the wise and crazed are one.<br/>
In the way of love, kin and strangers are one.<br/>
The one who they gave the wine of the beloved's union,<br/>
in his path, the Kaaba and house of idols are one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
</div>
</td></tr>
<tr style="vertical-align: top; font-size:90%">
<td>âQuatrain 305
</td>
<td style="padding-left: 2em;">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>According to the Quran, Muhammad is a mercy sent by God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> In regards to this, Rumi states:
</p>
<blockquote><p>"The Light of Muhammad does not abandon a Zoroastrian or Jew in the world. May the shade of his good fortune shine upon everyone! He brings all of those who are led astray into the Way out of the desert."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Rumi, however, asserts the supremacy of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> by stating:
</p>
<blockquote><p>"The Light of Muhammad has become a thousand branches (of knowledge), a thousand, so that both this world and the next have been seized from end to end. If Muhammad rips the veil open from a single such branch, thousands of monks and priests will tear the string of false belief from around their waists."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Many of Rumi's poems suggest the importance of outward religious observance and the primacy of the Qur'an.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Flee to God's Qur'an, take refuge in it<br/>
there with the spirits of the prophets merge.<br/>
The Book conveys the prophets' circumstances<br/>
those fish of the pure sea of Majesty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Rumi states:
</p>
<blockquote><div class="poem">
<p>I am the servant of the Qur'an as long as I have life.<br/>
I am the dust on the path of Muhammad, the Chosen one.<br/>
If anyone quotes anything except this from my sayings,<br/>
I am quit of him and outraged by these words.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
</div></blockquote>
<p>Rumi also states:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>"I "sewed" my two eyes shut from [desires for] this world and the next â this I learned from Muhammad."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>On the first page of the Masnavi, Rumi states:
</p>
<blockquote><p> "Hadha kitÃ¢bu 'l- mathnawÃ®y wa huwa uSÃ»lu uSÃ»li uSÃ»li 'd-dÃ®n wa kashshÃ¢fu 'l-qur'Ã¢n."<br/>
"This is the book of the Masnavi, and it is the roots of the roots of the roots of the (Islamic) Religion and it is the Explainer of the Qur'Ã¢n."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hadi_Sabzavari" title="Hadi Sabzavari">Hadi Sabzavari</a>, one of Iran's most important 19th-century philosophers, makes the following connection between the Masnavi and Islam, in the introduction to his philosophical commentary on the book:
</p>
<blockquote><p>It is a commentary on the versified exegesis [of the QurâÄn] and its occult mystery, since all of it [all of the MathnawÄ«] is, as you will see, an elucidation of the clear verses [of the QurâÄn], a clarification of prophetic utterances, a glimmer of the light of the luminous QurâÄn, and burning embers irradiating their rays from its shining lamp. As respects to hunting through the treasure-trove of the QurâÄn, one can find in it [the MathnawÄ«] all [the QurâÄn's] ancient philosophical wisdom; it [the MathnawÄ«] is all entirely eloquent philosophy. In truth, the pearly verse of the poem combines the Canon Law of Islam (<a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">sharÄ«Ê¿a</a>) with the Sufi Path (<a href="/wiki/Tariqa" title="Tariqa">á¹­arÄ«qa</a>) and the Divine Reality (<a href="/wiki/Haqiqa" title="Haqiqa">á¸¥aqÄ«qa</a>); the author's [RÅ«mÄ«] achievement belongs to God in his bringing together of the Law (sharÄ«Ê¿a), the Path, and the Truth in a way that includes critical intellect, profound thought, a brilliant natural temperament, and integrity of character that is endowed with power, insight, inspiration, and illumination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Seyyed_Hossein_Nasr" title="Seyyed Hossein Nasr">Seyyed Hossein Nasr</a> states:
</p>
<blockquote><p>One of the greatest living authorities on RÃ»mÃ® in Persia today, HÃ¢dÃ® HÃ¢'irÃ®, has shown in an unpublished work that some 6,000 verses of the DÃ®wÃ¢n and the MathnawÃ® are practically direct translations of Qur'Ã¢nic verses into Persian poetry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Rumi states in his <a href="/wiki/Diwan_(poetry)" title="Diwan (poetry)">DÄ«wÄn</a>:
</p>
<blockquote><p>The Sufi is hanging on to Muhammad, like <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>His <a href="/wiki/Masnavi" title="Masnavi">Masnavi</a> contains anecdotes and stories derived largely from the Quran and the hadith, as well as everyday tales.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Universality">Universality</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Shahram_Shiva" title="Shahram Shiva">Shahram Shiva</a> asserts that "Rumi is able to verbalise the highly personal and often confusing world of personal growth and development in a very clear and direct fashion. He does not offend anyone, and he includes everyone.... Today Rumi's poems can be heard in churches, synagogues, Zen monasteries, as well as in the downtown New York art/performance/music scene."
</p><p>To many modern Westerners, his teachings are one of the best introductions to the philosophy and practice of <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a>. In the West <a href="/wiki/Shahram_Shiva" title="Shahram Shiva">Shahram Shiva</a> has been teaching, performing and sharing the translations of the poetry of Rumi for nearly twenty years and has been instrumental in spreading Rumi's legacy in the English-speaking parts of the world.
</p><p>According to Professor Majid M. Naini,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> "Rumi's life and transformation provide true testimony and proof that people of all religions and backgrounds can live together in peace and harmony. Rumiâs visions, words, and life teach us how to reach inner peace and happiness so we can finally stop the continual stream of hostility and hatred and achieve true global peace and harmony.â
</p><p>Rumi's work has been translated into many of the world's languages, including Russian, German, Urdu, Turkish, Arabic, Bengali, French, Italian, and Spanish, and is being presented in a growing number of formats, including concerts, workshops, readings, dance performances, and other artistic creations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> The English interpretations of Rumi's poetry by <a href="/wiki/Coleman_Barks" title="Coleman Barks">Coleman Barks</a> have sold more than half a million copies worldwide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> and Rumi is one of the most widely read poets in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shahram_Shiva" title="Shahram Shiva">Shahram Shiva</a> book "Rending the Veil: Literal and Poetic Translations of Rumi" (1995, HOHM Press) is the recipient of the Benjamin Franklin Award.
</p><p>Recordings of Rumi poems have made it to the USA's Billboard's Top 20 list. A selection of American author <a href="/wiki/Deepak_Chopra" title="Deepak Chopra">Deepak Chopra</a>'s editing of the translations by Fereydoun Kia of Rumi's love poems has been performed by Hollywood personalities such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madonna_(entertainer)" title="Madonna (entertainer)">Madonna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goldie_Hawn" title="Goldie Hawn">Goldie Hawn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philip_Glass" title="Philip Glass">Philip Glass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Demi_Moore" title="Demi Moore">Demi Moore</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:5000_TL_A_reverse.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="209" data-file-width="422" decoding="async" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/5000_TL_A_reverse.jpg/220px-5000_TL_A_reverse.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/5000_TL_A_reverse.jpg/330px-5000_TL_A_reverse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c0/5000_TL_A_reverse.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:5000_TL_A_reverse.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rumi and his mausoleum on the reverse of the 5000 Turkish lira banknotes of 1981â1994</div></div></div>
<p>Rumi and his mausoleum were depicted on the <a href="/wiki/Obverse_and_reverse" title="Obverse and reverse">reverse</a> of the 5000 Turkish lira banknotes of 1981â1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is a famous landmark in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_India" title="Northern India">Northern India</a>, known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rumi_Gate" title="Rumi Gate">Rumi Gate</a>, situated in <a href="/wiki/Lucknow" title="Lucknow">Lucknow</a> (the capital of <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a>) named for Rumi. Indian filmmaker <a href="/wiki/Muzaffar_Ali" title="Muzaffar Ali">Muzaffar Ali</a> who is from Lucknow made a documentary, titled <i>Rumi in the Land of Khusrau</i> (2001), which presents concerts based on the works of Rumi and <a href="/wiki/Amir_Khusrau" title="Amir Khusrau">Amir Khusrau</a> and highlights parallels between the lives of the poets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iranian_world">Iranian world</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Ù¾Ø§Ø±Ø³Û Ú¯Ù Ú¯Ø±ÚÙ ØªØ§Ø²Û Ø®ÙØ´ØªØ± Ø§Ø³ØªÂ â Ø¹Ø´Ù Ø±Ø§ Ø®ÙØ¯ ØµØ¯ Ø²Ø¨Ø§Ù Ø¯ÛÚ¯Ø± Ø§Ø³Øª</p></blockquote>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Say it in Persian although in Arabic sounds betterâLove, however, has its own many other dialects</p></blockquote>
<p>These cultural, historical and linguistic ties between Rumi and <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> have made Rumi an iconic Iranian poet, and some of the most important Rumi scholars including Foruzanfar, Naini, Sabzewari, etc., have come from modern Iran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Franklin_Lewis_2000_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Franklin_Lewis_2000-98">[98]</a></sup> Rumi's poetry is displayed on the walls of many cities across <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, sung in Persian music,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Franklin_Lewis_2000_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-Franklin_Lewis_2000-98">[98]</a></sup> and read in school books.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rumi's poetry forms the basis of much classical <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Iran" title="Music of Iran">Iranian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Afghanistan" title="Music of Afghanistan">Afghan</a> music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Contemporary classical interpretations of his poetry are made by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_Reza_Shajarian" title="Muhammad Reza Shajarian">Muhammad Reza Shajarian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shahram_Nazeri" title="Shahram Nazeri">Shahram Nazeri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Davood_Azad" title="Davood Azad">Davood Azad</a> (the three from Iran) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ustad_Mohammad_Hashem_Cheshti" title="Ustad Mohammad Hashem Cheshti">Ustad Mohammad Hashem Cheshti</a> (Afghanistan).
</p>
<h3><span id="Mewlew.C4.AB_Sufi_Order.3B_Rumi_and_Turkey"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="MewlewÄ«_Sufi_Order;_Rumi_and_Turkey">MewlewÄ« Sufi Order; Rumi and Turkey</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Mevlevi_Order" title="Mevlevi Order">Mevlevi Order</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sama_(Sufism)" title="Sama (Sufism)">Sama (Sufism)</a></div>
<p>The MewlewÄ« Sufi order was founded in 1273 by Rumi's followers after his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> His first successor in the rectorship of the order was "<a href="/wiki/Husam_al-Din_Chalabi" title="Husam al-Din Chalabi">Husam Chalabi</a>" himself, after whose death in 1284 Rumi's younger and only surviving son, <a href="/wiki/Sultan_Walad" title="Sultan Walad">Sultan Walad</a> (died 1312), popularly known as author of the mystical <i>Maá¹­nawÄ« RabÄbnÄma</i>, or the <i>Book of the Rabab</i> was installed as grand master of the order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> The leadership of the order has been kept within Rumi's family in Konya uninterruptedly since then.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
The MewlewÄ« Sufis, also known as Whirling Dervishes, believe in performing their <i><a href="/wiki/Dhikr" title="Dhikr">dhikr</a></i> in the form of <a href="/wiki/Sama_(Sufism)" title="Sama (Sufism)">Sama</a>. During the time of Rumi (as attested in the <i>ManÄqib ul-ÄrefÄ«n</i> of AflÄkÄ«), his followers gathered for musical and "turning" practices.
</p><p>According to tradition, Rumi was himself a notable musician who played the <a href="/wiki/Rebab" title="Rebab"><i>robÄb</i></a>, although his favourite instrument was the <i><a href="/wiki/Ney" title="Ney">ney</a></i> or reed flute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-order_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-order-105">[105]</a></sup> The music accompanying the <i>samÄÊ¿</i> consists of settings of poems from the <i>Maá¹­nawÄ«</i> and <i>DÄ«wÄn-e KabÄ«r</i>, or of Sultan Walad's poems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-order_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-order-105">[105]</a></sup> The MawlawÄ«yah was a well-established Sufi order in the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, and many of the members of the order served in various official positions of the Caliphate. The centre for the Mevlevi was in Konya. There is also a MewlewÄ« monastery (<span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">Ø¯Ø±Ú¯Ø§Ù</span></span>, <i>dargÄh</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a> near the <a href="/wiki/Galata_Tower" title="Galata Tower">Galata Tower</a> in which the <i>samÄÊ¿</i> is performed and accessible to the public. The MewlewÄ« order issues an invitation to people of all backgrounds:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1023981488">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .rquote{width:auto!important;float:none!important}}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote rquote" style="float: right; width: 33%;"><div class="poem">
<p><i>Come, come, whoever you are,</i><br/>
<i>Wanderer, idolater, worshiper of fire,</i><br/>
<i>Come even though you have broken your vows a thousand times,</i><br/>
<i>Come, and come yet again.</i><br/>
<i>Ours is not a caravan of despair.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
</div></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Turkey.Konya008.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="815" data-file-width="1141" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Turkey.Konya008.jpg/220px-Turkey.Konya008.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Turkey.Konya008.jpg/330px-Turkey.Konya008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Turkey.Konya008.jpg/440px-Turkey.Konya008.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Turkey.Konya008.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rumi's tomb in <a href="/wiki/Konya" title="Konya">Konya</a>, Turkey.</div></div></div>
<p>During Ottoman times, the Mevlevi produced a number of notable poets and musicians, including Sheikh Ghalib, Ismail Rusuhi Dede of Ankara, Esrar Dede, Halet Efendi, and Gavsi Dede, who are all buried at the Galata MewlewÄ« KhÄna (Turkish: <i>Mevlevi-Hane</i>) in Istanbul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Music, especially that of the ney, plays an important part in the Mevlevi.
</p><p>With the foundation of the modern, secular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_Turkey" title="Republic of Turkey">Republic of Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Kemal_Atat%C3%BCrk" title="Mustafa Kemal AtatÃ¼rk">Mustafa Kemal AtatÃ¼rk</a> removed religion from the sphere of public policy and restricted it exclusively to that of personal morals, behaviour and faith. On 13 December 1925, a law was passed closing all the <i><a href="/wiki/Khanqah" title="Khanqah">tekke</a></i>s (dervish lodges) and <i>zÄwiyas</i> (chief dervish lodges), and the centres of veneration to which visits (<i>ziyÄrat</i>) were made. Istanbul alone had more than 250 <i>tekke</i>s as well as small centres for gatherings of various fraternities; this law dissolved the Sufi Orders, prohibited the use of mystical names, titles and costumes pertaining to their titles, impounded the Orders' assets, and banned their ceremonies and meetings. The law also provided penalties for those who tried to re-establish the Orders. Two years later, in 1927, the Mausoleum of MevlÃ¢na in Konya was allowed to reopen as a Museum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1950s, the Turkish government began allowing the Whirling Dervishes to perform once a year in Konya. The MewlÄnÄ festival is held over two weeks in December; its culmination is on 17 December, the Urs of MewlÄnÄ (anniversary of Rumi's death), called <i>Å abe ArÅ«s</i> (Ø´Ø¨ Ø¹Ø±ÙØ³) (Persian meaning "nuptial night"), the night of Rumi's union with God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urs_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-urs-109">[109]</a></sup> In 1974, the Whirling Dervishes were permitted to travel to the West for the first time. In 2005, <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> proclaimed "The <a href="/wiki/Mevlevi_Order" title="Mevlevi Order">Mevlevi</a> <a href="/wiki/Sama_(Sufism)" title="Sama (Sufism)">Sama</a> Ceremony" of <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> as one of the <a href="/wiki/Masterpieces_of_the_Oral_and_Intangible_Heritage_of_Humanity" title="Masterpieces of the Oral and Intangible Heritage of Humanity">Masterpieces of the Oral and Intangible Heritage of Humanity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_denomination">Religious denomination</span></h3>
<p>As <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_G._Browne" title="Edward G. Browne">Edward G. Browne</a> noted, the three most prominent mystical Persian poets Rumi, <a href="/wiki/Sanai" title="Sanai">Sanai</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Farid_al-Din_Attar" title="Farid al-Din Attar">Attar</a> were all Sunni Muslims and their poetry abounds with praise for the first two caliphs <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umar_ibn_al-Khatt%C4%81b" title="Umar ibn al-KhattÄb">Umar ibn al-KhattÄb</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Annemarie_Schimmel" title="Annemarie Schimmel">Annemarie Schimmel</a>, the tendency among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shia" title="Shia">Shia</a> authors to anachronistically include leading mystical poets such as Rumi and Attar among their own ranks, became stronger after the introduction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelver_Shia" title="Twelver Shia">Twelver Shia</a> as the state religion in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safavid_Empire" title="Safavid Empire">Safavid Empire</a> in 1501.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eight_hundredth_anniversary_celebrations">Eight hundredth anniversary celebrations</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mawlana_Rumi_-_Stamp_Afghanistan_1968.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="425" data-file-width="311" decoding="async" height="301" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Mawlana_Rumi_-_Stamp_Afghanistan_1968.jpg/220px-Mawlana_Rumi_-_Stamp_Afghanistan_1968.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/14/Mawlana_Rumi_-_Stamp_Afghanistan_1968.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mawlana_Rumi_-_Stamp_Afghanistan_1968.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rumi in Stamp of Afghanistan, 1968</div></div></div>
<p>In Afghanistan, Rumi is known as <i>MawlÄnÄ</i>, in Turkey as <i>MevlÃ¢na</i>, and in Iran as <i>MolavÄ«</i>.
</p><p>At the proposal of the Permanent Delegations of Afghanistan, Iran, and Turkey, and as approved by its executive board and General Conference in conformity with its mission of "constructing in the minds of men the defences of peace", <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> was associated with the celebration, in 2007, of the eight hundredth anniversary of Rumi's birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> The commemoration at UNESCO itself took place on 6 September 2007;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNESCO_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-UNESCO-2">[2]</a></sup> UNESCO issued a medal in Rumi's name in the hope that it would prove an encouragement to those who are engaged in research on and dissemination of Rumi's ideas and ideals, which would, in turn, enhance the diffusion of the ideals of UNESCO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNESDOC_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNESDOC-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 30 September 2007, Iranian school bells were rung throughout the country in honour of Mewlana.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Also in that year, Iran held a Rumi Week from 26 October to 2 November. An international ceremony and conference were held in <a href="/wiki/Tehran" title="Tehran">Tehran</a>; the event was opened by the Iranian president and the chairman of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iranian_parliament" title="Iranian parliament">Iranian parliament</a>. Scholars from twenty-nine countries attended the events, and 450 articles were presented at the conference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Iranian musician <a href="/wiki/Shahram_Nazeri" title="Shahram Nazeri">Shahram Nazeri</a> was awarded the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L%C3%A9gion_d%27honneur" title="LÃ©gion d'honneur">LÃ©gion d'honneur</a> and Iran's House of Music Award in 2007 for his renowned works on Rumi masterpieces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> 2007 was declared as the "International Rumi Year" by UNESCO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>Also on 30 September 2007, Turkey celebrated Rumi's eight-hundredth birthday with a giant Whirling Dervish ritual performance of the <i>samÄÊ¿</i>, which was televised using forty-eight cameras and broadcast live in eight countries. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ertugrul_Gunay" title="Ertugrul Gunay">Ertugrul Gunay</a>, of the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Culture_and_Tourism_(Turkey)" title="Ministry of Culture and Tourism (Turkey)">Ministry of Culture and Tourism</a>, stated, "Three hundred dervishes are scheduled to take part in this ritual, making it the largest performance of sema in history."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mawlana_Rumi_Review">Mawlana Rumi Review</span></h3>
<p>The <i>Mawlana Rumi Review</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> is published annually by The Centre for Persian and Iranian Studies at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Exeter" title="University of Exeter">University of Exeter</a> in collaboration with The Rumi Institute in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicosia,_Cyprus" title="Nicosia, Cyprus">Nicosia, Cyprus</a>, and Archetype Books<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archetypebooks.com_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-archetypebooks.com-121">[121]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">Cambridge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archetypebooks.com_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-archetypebooks.com-121">[121]</a></sup> The first volume was published in 2010, and it has come out annually since then. According to the principal editor of the journal, Leonard Lewisohn: "Although a number of major Islamic poets easily rival the likes of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dante" title="Dante">Dante</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shakespeare" title="Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Milton" title="John Milton">Milton</a> in importance and output, they still enjoy only a marginal literary fame in the West because the works of Arabic and Persian thinkers, writers and poets are considered as negligible, frivolous, tawdry sideshows beside the grand narrative of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Canon" title="Western Canon">Western Canon</a>. It is the aim of the Mawlana Rumi Review to redress this carelessly inattentive approach to <a href="/wiki/World_literature" title="World literature">world literature</a>, which is something far more serious than a minor faux pas committed by the Western literary imagination."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
